B001JAFYFG|3.0|\\"Logan's Run\\" (1976), recently released to Blu-ray, has been around for a long time in the earlier formats and is generally regarded as an OK but not great sci-fi film.  It's a fairly interesting story of a future society -- \\"sometime in the twenty-third century\\" -- living luxuriously in a huge city under a sealed dome.  The citizens have been conditioned to believe that shortly before reaching the age of 30 they are eligible to be reborn (\\"renewed\\") by participating in a sort of religious ritual known as \\"Carousel\\" in which, after being bodily destroyed, they would be \\"renewed\\" as a baby to start a new life.  The hook here, of course, is that they are not renewed at all but are simply eliminated as a means to keep the population constant and in balance. This is what is known as the \\"gimmick.\\"  Any citizen trying to escape from the dome is designated to be a \\"runner\\" and is hunted down by government agents called \\"Sandmen\\" and, if apprehended, immediately killed which puts an abrupt end to his or her evening plans.<br /><br />The story bears a resemblance to the much later Michael Bay film \\"The Island\\" (2005) and Arthur C. Clarke's famous novel, \\"The City and the Stars\\" which I've always thought would make a very good picture.  It's fairly easy to guess how the film turns out, but I won't spoil it for those who haven't seen it, although there can't be too many of them by now.<br /><br />What I had forgotten was that this film was photographed in Todd-AO wide-screen and looks absolutely splendid in the Blu-ray edition.  The film's special effects are limited pretty much to the nicely fashioned miniatures of \\"The City.\\"  The only fly in the ointment there is that the water in the lakes of the city ripples too quickly and immediately gives it away as being a miniature.  If these scenes had been shot using a high speed camera that problem could have been minimized.  However it's possible that high-speed Todd-AO cameras were not available. The film's strong points are the imaginatively designed sets and a good cast: Michael York, Jenny Agutter, Richard Jordan, Peter Ustinov, Farrah Fawcett-Majors and Roscoe Lee Browne.  Michael Anderson, Jr., the director's son also appears briefly in the film.  There is a nice orchestral score augmented by electronic sounds by Jerry Goldsmith.  I had seen the film a few times in the earlier formats, but the Blu-ray version completely \\"renewed\\" my appreciation of it.

B0041KKYCY|2.0|The remake is extremely difficult to watch but it is not because of the simulated rape, torture and final death scenes. It is because you are going to have to suspend belief like crazy to overlook all the plot holes.<br /><br />Firstly, there is the casting of Butler for our protaganist. She doesn't look like a writer, she doesn<br />t act like a writer, she doesn't talk like a writer. Let's face it she is completely unconvincing as a writer. Keaton has the perfect combination of fairly average 'plain Jane' looks and personality with just enough of the shy conservative introspection that writers are known for. The whole point of being a writer is to be able to escape reality and create fantasy on the page. Butler looks more like she works in a gym full-time, is far too extraverted and outgoing in personality and turns up to a completely isolated cabin in the middle of knowhere with enough boxes of booze for her entire stay and not a single book amongst it. Writers never go anywhere without at least one book. They are either writing a book, reading a book or trying to do both simultaneously. At least she could have brought a Chicago Manual of Style with her.<br /><br />If we don't believe Butler is a writer then how the hell are we supposed to believe she is in the middle of nowhere writing a manuscript?<br /><br />The next problem is her claim that she is looking to get away for some peace and quiet. Away from what exactly? In no time at all, she is talking on her cellphone to someone, somewhere about whatever. Not only that, she brings a laptop hooked up to the internet with her in order to write this 'magnificently groundbreaking' manuscript to make Moby Dick read like a fishing story at the local creek and for some bizarre reason she can't finish it where she lives. Ever heard of a library? Are we really expected to believe that there is absolutely no way she could finish this book unless she travels all the way to this archaic cabin in the middle of nowhere? Even if the story is about a protaganist who is actually staying in a completely isolated cabin in the sticks, can't she just imagine it anyway? Or are we to believe that Wells defended the Earth from Martians with bacteria and then simply wrote down what happened verbatim and called it War of The Worlds?<br /><br />The contradiction to this is that when she gets to the cabin she hits writer's block anyway and decides to get smashed on weed and booze.<br /><br />Next, we are expected to believe that she is single. Butler playing the single Hills, simply does not work.<br /><br />The change from four to five antagonists in this remake was a good idea because it allows for some use of leverage on the part of Hills. Hills sends the tape of the crimes that all five men have committed but we never find out whether this is the only copy. If it is a dub then Jennifer must have the original but how did she do this without another tape or the necessary equipment? If it is the original, then his pregnant wife although horrified to find out what her late husband has done, is still a widow because of what Hills has done in retaliation and now has in her possession the only piece of evidence.<br /><br />There is no police report and without the tape there is no evidence that the rapes actually occured except her own testimony against being charged with the murder of five men. Jennifer even seems to take on a ghostly otherworldly presence where she seems to be able to appear and disappear at will.<br /><br />If we are to believe that the rapes actually did occur then the rest of the movie must be absolutely grounded in reality. Everything appears to be as close to perfect as it can be until Nick's beautiful wife disappears. Where is Amy Dunne? And what, if anything, is Nick's role in her disappearance?<br /><br />\\"Love makes you want to be a better man. But maybe love, real love, also gives you permission to just be the man you are.\\" - Nick Dunne<br /><br />**Mild Spoiler Alert**<br /><br />Gone Girl starts off slow, gains tons of momentum, and then ends horribly. With that said, I didn't hate the book. In fact, I finished a good portion of it in one day.<br /><br />I appreciated the fact that Gillian Flynn was able to keep me guessing and on the edge of my seat. As I read, my opinion of both characters constantly cycled between extremes. Both Nick and Amy both have secrets that are explosive, and the web of lies that they leave behind make it difficult to determine who is truly the victim. Once the plot begins to gain momentum, it's very difficult to put the book down. It's in the end, in my opinion, that Flynn drops the ball and the brilliance ends. She spends a lot of time winding you up for a dramatic and just ending, and then leaves you hanging. When I read the last line of the book, I actually turned the page expecting to read another chapter. I found the acknowledgements instead. I actually re-read the last page, thinking that I must have missed something important. In my opinion, the ending ruins an otherwise brilliantly written book.<br /><br />If you enjoy stories that lack closure, then you will likely appreciate this read. In some ways, Gone Girl reminds me of Defending Jacob. The premises are conceptually similar, but the endings, in my opinion, set them apart. Defending Jacob offered some form of closure while also leaving the rest up for interpretation. Gone Girl, on the other hand, leaves nothing up for interpretation and offers no form of closure whatsoever. You walk away from this novel with the knowledge that what happened to this family will happen again. Jeez, there's nothing to do but have a beer this morning, to put off making a dinner reservation. I hate ironing, but I'm doing it for poor hubby, so he'll remember how wonderful I am. Golly, maybe we should have a baby, or maybe not?<br /><br />I stopped reading the book, feeling that no matter what came after the first third of it would be a continuing mind-numbing waste of my time.<br /><br />Seeing this book getting hotter reviews , moving to the top of the best seller lists and staying there was mystifying. So, I tried again. More dull inaction, with a slow peeling away of nasty character flaws that made me even more annoyed that anyone could possibly care about this story.<br /><br />Whammo. If you can get past the first half of the book, you're in for the treat of the year! Brilliant plotting, on-the-edge-of-your-seat psychological suspense, a tensely riveting climax, and a deserved ending will greet you. But, you'll still hate the characters. It's the character(s?) that will come after -- and hopefully, there will not be a sequel -- for whom you will have extraordinary sympathy.

0525478817|5.0|In a strange way, I always sort of dread reading anything by John Green. Weird, right? See, I live in perpetual fear that one day he will let me down and his book will be less brilliant than I'm expecting. My expectations when it comes to John Green are ridiculously high, because, basically, he's like the god of the nerds and, even in the books I like less, his writing makes me laugh and makes me feel. Despite this constant fear, his books have, so far, improved every time, with The Fault in Our Stars being his best yet, dark and funny and honest and touching and hopeful and depressing and painful and perfect.<br /><br />*wipes away tears*<br /><br />When people talk about this book, the first thing they mention is the sadness of it, of how many tissues are requisite to getting through this novel without emerging a snot monster at the end. What struck me much more strongly, though, was the sense of humor and optimism running through the book, even the darkest moments. The humor doesn't subtract from the pain or the suffering; it humanizes it, and enhances the strength of the characters.<br /><br />In the past, my main complaint about John Green's books (no, I did not think they were all completely perfect) were the characters. They were real, but they were annoying and repetitive (especially with Paper Towns and Looking for Alaska. None of them did I fall completely in love with, although Tiny Cooper was close. In Hazel and Augusta, John Green's characterization has seriously hit its stride in my opinion.<br /><br />Hazel's voice is just...incredible. Her sarcasm, her brutal honesty, her anger, her intelligence and her wit all made her one of the most wonderful narrators I've ever encountered. I love the way she phrases things. I love that she uses some words that I don't know the meaning of, which really doesn't happen often in my reading. Hazel feels wholly like a kindred spirit, like we would be friends if we ever met, which would totally never happen since we're both so anti-social. Also, she feels one hundred percent like a female to me, always a remarkable thing for a male author to accomplish.<br /><br />Hazel and Augustus meet and there's an immediate attraction. Though they immediately feel for one another on some level, John Green deftly doesn't go anywhere near instalove territory. For one thing, there's Hazel's cancer, which holds the two apart. Even if there weren't and they immediately leaped into a relationship, I would have been okay with it, because the two legitimately develop a bond. They wisecrack and have this insane rapport; they share a love of word play and navel gazing. They trade their favorite novels and both go into the experience with an open mind, even though the novels were not what they were expecting. They are, without a doubt, one of the best, most convincing, most well-matched couples I have encountered in fiction.<br /><br />Throughout The Fault in Our Stars, John Green has woven a lot of thoughts about the value of fiction and about what a novel really is, both to the reader and to the author. Hazel's favorite novel, An Imperial Affliction by Peter Van Houten, is a work of literary fiction about a young girl with cancer; Augustus' is a series of novelizations based on a video game about the adventures of Staff Sergeant Max Mayhem. While there's clearly a difference of literary merit between the two, I loved how John Green stressed the importance of both.<br /><br />I feel like there isn't much more that I can say about this without cutting into your enjoyment of the book. I realize I didn't talk about the cancer at all, but I suppose that was intentional, because the book isn't so much about the cancer as about the people who are partially made of cancer. The cancer is them after all, not a foreign element. The book is wholly about cancer and wholly not, though I know that probably doesn't make any sense. What I mean is that this is in no way simply some weepy tearjerker stereotype of a cancer book.<br /><br />My words are failing me, so I leave you now with this: The Fault in Our Stars is everything I dreamed it would be and more. John Green constantly increases his awesomeness. With this novel, he will break your heart, repair said heart a few sizes larger, make you laugh, give you hope, rip your heart to pieces again, and basically tell you the utter truth about a lot of awful things also known as life.cer book.<br /><br />My words are failing me, so I leave you now with this: The Fault in Our Stars is everything I dreamed it would be and more. John Green constantly increases his awesomeness. With this novel, he will break your heart, repair said heart a few sizes larger, make you laugh, give you hope, rip your heart to pieces again, and basically tell you the utter truth about a lot of awful things also known as life.

As opposed to other books this book is less about helping and treating other fairly and more about fulfilling your own personal legend. The boys time in the desert with a mystical alchemist is a time in the book filled with great quotes and powerful views on lives and how we feel about accomplishing our dreams. We are told it is better to attempt something and fail than to not make an attempt and go on living life fearing failure. This idea and others define Coelho's thoughts toward life and the lessons he seems to have learned firsthand. His writing style is very compatible with sending messages, it is straightforward and blunt. Nothing is left up to guesswork . If he is trying to get a point or a idea across it is very clear. The life lessons are delivered clearly and very often which leads to a very moving and thought provoking book.<br />  However, as valuable as it is to take the ideas seriously and try to apply them to our lives, the sincerity and power of the teachings are lost with the massive amounts of repetition throughout the book. Coupled with a plot that seems tailored to teach and not to tell an deep or intriguing story these morals can become somewhat lost and hard to relate to.  Throughout the book the boy meets several shallow and underdeveloped characters for short amounts of time. They all seem to repeat the same message in different or similar forms. From the King to the Englishman to the Alchemist they all tell the boy to not be afraid to fulfill his dreams and that the Soul of the World will help him along. All of the characters are not even given proper names and fly in and out of the story before we can learn a little more about them. They are all envelopes for the same letter and repeat themselves multiple times. Only one character seems to have been excluded from this mold and that would be the crystal merchant. The boy learns many lessons from the merchant without them being hurled at both him and the reader. It takes a while but eventually we do learn a little bit about the glass merchant and his thoughts and his past. The glass merchant is an example of one who is afraid to pursue his dreams and is somewhat a contrasting personality from the boy. It is a nice break to indirectly learn a lesson and have some freedom to interpret it the way you would like, not how the author thinks it should be viewed. The plot does become unrealistic as the book goes on, the boy gains seemingly magical powers and is forced to turn himself into the wind. The lessons taught in these times seem hard to relate to. The plot seems geared towards only morals and not towards telling an engrossing and exciting story. This caused me some boredom but the core of the plot was enjoyable.<br /> Overall it is good to listen to this book and what it has to say but not look past that too much. Don't expect an enthralling tale that will have you reading for hours.

0553381407|4.0|If you've read any psychology self-help books in the past about dysfunctional families, then you will recognize much of the ground this book covers. It has much of the currently accepted insights about recovering from toxic families, and has them written in a very straightforward, actionable manner. Where this book differs is that it offers more concrete suggestions when coming up with a recovery plan than some of the other books I read. Another major difference is that it deviates from most other books in two key areas: unlike other books, this one says that forgiveness is not necessary or key for moving on. Although it does agree with the accepted wisdom that revenge and bitterness is fruitless, it makes a convincing case about why that doesn't mean one has to go as far as to forgive one's parents either. The other area where it differs from many other self-help books is that it considers confrontation of one's parents to be very necessary, rather than an optional step or a step one should avoid altogether. I agree with both these views, especially the confrontation part.<br /><br />It doesn't go as deeply into the psychodynamics analysis and discussions of the roots of dysfunctional families as some other books are, because it is more focused on conveying actionable recovery strategies in plain English. Whether this is a plus or minus to you depends on what you want from a book. If you like deep psychodynamic discussion or psychoanalysis, this is not for you. But what the book does do, it does very well.

0691152640|2.0|This book provides simple descriptive statistics and summaries of various types of financial crises, using new data. I called it a disappointment for two reasons. The authors provide simple analysis that, as they acknowledge, provide no real causal insights - they are just showing simple correlations and patterns. They could have tested some real hypotheses using even modestly more advanced methods. Alternatively, they could have provided more qualitative information with compelling descriptions of how crises emerged and evolved, with case studies of key crises. For me, their middle of the road approach failed to satisfy a desire for comparative insight from case studies as well as a desire for sophisticated and careful data analysis. In the end, I learned a few things from patterns in their dataset, but they could have delivered most of the value-added in perhaps 50 pages, not 300.

0374275637|3.0|Daniel Kahneman penned one of the more intriguing science books of 2011 in _Thinking Fast and Slow_. A cognitive psychologist, he applied his craft to the field of economics and won a Nobel Prize for his development of Prospect Theory, which accounts for an overlooked emotional aspect to rational economic choices.<br /><br />_Thinking Fast and Slow_ is part memoir, part meditation on science, and part textbook. While it was one of the most lauded books of 2011, that \\"part-ness\\" is a major problem with the book: Author Kahneman simply can't decide what he's writing, and the book is less effective for its split personality. The memoir reflects fondly upon the author's many scientific collaborations, the meditation on science offers a love letter of sorts to the nature of inquiry and discovery, and the textbook looks at how people interpret their world, especially when either (or both) the intuitive and rational aspects of human thought fail. That latter part is the most effective; Kahneman looks at the ways in which people make quick decisions based on statistical data and how that data can mess with their minds.<br /><br />That the book can't focus on one of its personalities leaves it less effective than a bestseller it compares itself against, Malcolm Gladwell's _Blink_. In addition, Kahneman makes frequent references to Nassim Nicholas Taleb's _The Black Swan_, a book with a format similar to Kahneman's, but which comes together much more effectively.<br /><br />Though _Thinking Fast and Slow_ can be a fascinating read when it mimics Gladwell, it falls down badly when aspiring to be Taleb too. On the whole, it's a book of fascinating facts, but also an often tedious, repetitive, and unfocused slog. The sum ends up less effective than the parts.<br /><br />Like so many books today, this one could have used a more aggressive editor, someone who could have shaped and directed it toward a more fruitful outcome. Yes, it can be interesting, but as a popular science book it lacks the breezy style of Gladwell or the almost surreal feel of Taleb. And that makes what could have been a great book merely okay.it lacks the breezy style of Gladwell or the almost surreal feel of Taleb. And that makes what could have been a great book merely okay.

Whitechapel is one of the few places where the Blue Bloods don't have complete control, instead Blade a mysterious Rogue rules over them and he seems to have taken an interest in Honoria, but can he be trusted, or will he just use them as a pawn to get the revenge he desires over the Blue Bloods he hates?<br /><br />Normally I am a little suspicious of books that have kick ass covers, the quality of the content doesn't always match the beautiful wrapping, but I am pleased to say that with this book, it was fantastic both inside and outside, I. Now I haven't actually read huge amounts of Steampunk so when I say this world was completely original bare that in mind! But it was a great mixture of historical with the setting being in a Victorianesque and paranormal with the presence of Blue Bloods, who were essentially a new and interesting take on vampires. I also liked that the Steampunk element wasn't over the top as although there were nifty gadgets being used and mechanical men, there wasn't women swaggering around in strippy tights, corsets and funny hats every 5 minutes and lots of weird and outlandish technology that needed to be overly explained. So I am definitely looking forward to the next book so I can explore this world further and delve into the more intricate aspects now that the basics have been set up.<br /><br />Now Blade...he has promptly been added to my list of book boyfriends, a very bad boy who has a soft spot for those he considers his family and of course the woman he loves and a tortured past, how was I supposed to resist that? But you can't swing a cat in the romance world and not hit a hero like that, but Blade had that extra something that had me panting, darkly sensual and struggling with inner demons but determined to do right by the people he protected I was doomed to love him.  Honoria I did like, but she took some time for me to warm to and although she was a good heroine it wasn't her that stood out for me.<br /><br />This book was well written, with an interesting and well crafted world with plenty of characters from some of the vile Blue Bloods to Blade's motley crew for you to get involved with, and I can't wait for the next book to come out and continue the story.<br /><br />*ARC provided by publishers I supposed to resist that? B0076NKNLA|4.0|Although I have enjoyed my share of Marilyn Monroe movies, I've never been obsessed with her or made it my business to read biographical info on her.  I certainly know she died young, was married to DiMaggio & Miller, etc.  Thus, I cannot speak even remotely to the truthfulness of MY WEEK WITH MARILYN.  I can say, however, that it was an interesting film that engaged my interest throughout, and that it features an eye-opening performance from Michelle Williams.<br /><br />MY WEEK WITH MARILYN covers the time period when Marilyn came over to England to star in Laurence Olivier's PRINCE AND THE SHOWGIRL.  Olivier (Kenneth Branagh) was directing and co-starring, and felt that Monroe would guarantee a big hit.  For me, as someone who worked for YEARS in theater, I was in many ways most interested in seeing the clash of acting styles between Monroe & Olivier.  Monroe was trying to be a method actor (and she travelled with her own acting coach)...which sometimes seems like a crutch she is using when she's feeling insecure.  Olivier was more old-school, where acting was more about craft and instinct and timing and delivery.  When the two great movie stars come together, sparks fly.  But they aren't the sparks that create movie magic...they create friction and frustration.  I enjoyed watching all the British actors struggling to deal with Monroe's \\"method\\" of acting.  (Best of the bunch was watching Judi Dench, doing all she could to help Marilyn...she gives a delightful performance in a tiny role.)<br /><br />The movie is told from the point of view of Third Assistant Director Colin Clark (on whose memoir the film is based).  Clark is a young man, new to the film business, who essentially is the gopher for Olivier and everyone else involved in the film.  He forges an unlikely friendship with Monroe, who briefly comes to trust him more than anyone else around.  This alliance lasts about a week (hence the title), where Colin is practically living with Monroe, even sleeping in her bed to offer comfort.  There are broad hints that some of Monroe's erratic behavior is due to drugs, but for the most part, the film portrays her as very insecure and lonely and misunderstood.  While the film doesn't shy away from the ugly aspects of her life, it is safe to say they are left unexplored.<br /><br />The film is a fairly loving tribute to Monroe.  While she can be difficult, she is always shown fairly sympathetically.  Actually, almost everyone in the film is sympathetic.  Olivier occasionally blusters and acts out...but honestly, we can understand his frustration.  Overall, he comes off well.  The movie FEELS evenhanded and humane...it's a nice mood.<br /><br />Best of all is Michelle Williams.  She's done good work before, but much of it has felt SIMILAR.  She seems to play contemporary women grappling with some level of demons.  (BLUE VALENTINE, BROKEBACK MOUNTAIN).  Her characters may have inner strength (MEEK'S CUTOFF, WENDY AND LUCY), but they aren't usually dynamic, charismatic, loud women.  In MY WEEK WITH MARILYN, we see a side of her that has not been much on display, and my estimation of her jumped up accordingly.  Her Marilyn is touching and grappling with demons, but she is also sexy and wildly charismatic.  When Williams portrays the \\"public\\" Marilyn, handling rabid fans or a crazy press conference...she acts so \\"non-Michelle Williams-like\\" that I was quite impressed.  Her many accolades were much deserved.  Branagh also does a great Olivier.  It's perhaps a bit more of an impression that a fully-realized performance...but he's charming and convincing and clearly having a ball with the role.  Others don't fare quite as well.  Emma Watson is bland (thanks to the script) as Colin's girlfriend; Dougray Scott doesn't get to do much as Arthur Miller and Julia Ormond is miscast as Olivier's wife, Vivien Leigh.<br /><br />This is not a deeply revelatory film, but it is highly entertaining and the time flies by.  The art & costume directionis flawless, including the reconstructions of many scenes from Monroe films.  It's a feel-good film about a real life person who was nearly on the brink of self-destruction.  That's contradictory, I know...but that's how the film felt.  It is certainly worth a look.is flawless, including the reconstructions of many scenes from Monroe films.  It's a feel-good film about a real life person who was nearly on the brink of self-destruction.  That's contradictory, I know...but that's how the film felt.  It is certainly worth a look.

B007C4ZJ3K|2.0|HAYWIRE was a deeply disappointing movie.  Steven Soderbergh has helmed some truly outstanding movies over the year, and when I heard that he was making his own action-thriller with a great cast...I was pretty pumped.<br /><br />My first hint that this movie might not be great was in his casting of Gina Carano, a well-known MMA star, in the lead.  She brought limited acting experience to the film, which Soderbergh essentially crafted for her.  This is similar to the way in which he created GIRLFRIEND EXPERIENCE to showcase porn star Sasha Gray in a \\"legit\\" role.  The movie was well-nigh unwatchable, and Gray was okay at best.  Carano is also okay at best.  She's supposed to be carrying an entire movie on her shoulders, but through no fault of her own, she isn't much of an actor.  She's got the action moves down, and I certainly believed she could kick the butt of all these male adversaries...but when required to show emotion, or humor, or determination or any recognizable emotion...her skills were pretty tiny.  Again, Soderbergh apparently saw \\"movie star\\" in her somewhere and crafted this project for her.  I don't blame her for letting him...but I also doubt we'll be seeing her in many lead roles in the near future.<br /><br />HAYWIRE tells the story of a black-ops agent (Carano) who has apparently been targeted for elimination by someone she knows as an ally.  The movie tells its story out-of-order...and frankly, I'm not sure there's a good reason for that.  It makes the story needlessly complicated, yet adds NOTHING to the suspense.  We're essentially going from one fight scene to another, and the plot is so thin and the explanations so sketchy...all we know is that we should be rooting FOR her and against anyone trying to kill her.  I suppose I could have given more effort to sorting out the plot specifics...but the movie is so murkily filmed and so un-involving from the get-go that I had no impetus for working that hard.<br /><br />I had hoped for a guilty-pleasure film...but I'm astonished to say that even a mediocre Jason Statham movie will give you more vicarious action thrills, and will skip the artsy pretense.  There is certainly a case to be made for \\"artsy\\" genre thrillers (DRIVE springs to mind)...but HAYWIRE is just boring.  Carano has some pretty high profile co-stars, but their roles are either tiny and undemanding (Bill Paxton, Michael Douglass, Antonio Banderas) or the actor can barely muster up any intensity for their role (Ewan McGregor is bland as Carano's boss and Michael Fassbender seems vaguely embarrassed to be in the film).  Channing Tatum generates momentary interest...but his dialogue is also mush-mouthed at times.<br /><br />It's tempting to give this film one-star, but I'll stick with two because if nothing else, the one-on-one fight scenes ring with some authenticity...people actually seem to get hurt.  I think Carano could be a nice small part of a bigger film...something like THE EXPENDIBLES, perhaps.  But she is no star...no matter how well she kicks a*s.

B00005MKA3|5.0|After many listens to this wonderful album it became clear to me that modern Hip Hop owes a debt of thanks to Bob Marley in many ways - &#34;Survival&#34; is one such example.  This classic record plays out like a true concept album; from the title, album cover (depicting flags of troubled Black nations around the world) to the song titles and lyrics, all screaming for the unification of anyone with African blood regardless of geographic location.  &#34;Africa Unite&#34; is the most potent example of this ideaology.  I feel as if every great rap album illustrating similar concepts has to have its roots in &#34;Survival&#34;.  One clearly evident example is the whistle-sounding keyboard lines at the beginning of &#34;So Much Trouble in the World&#34;.  Sound familiar?  Its the same effect employed in classic fashion by Dr. Dre and Snoop Dog on their early classics.  Bob Marley's original ghetto was Trench Town and his early work called for the rising up of the ghetto class; as he spent more time traveling and exploring different parts of the world, he expanded his idea of the &#34;ghetto&#34; - no longer just the rough streets of Jamaica, but anywhere Blacks are treated as second class citizens.  This album represents the pinnacle of that journey.  &#34;One Drop&#34; is Marley and family at their most gospel.  Try listening to this song and not feeling the pure joy Bob emits while reminding his people to be proud of who they are, even if, in the case of a &#34;One Drop&#34;, they have &#34;mixed&#34; blood.  Track by track, one can't help but draw parallels between this record and the great Hip Hop albums of our time.  NWA's &#34;Straight Outta Compton&#34; is the rap equivilant of Bob Marley and the Wailers first successful international album release &#34;Catch a Fire&#34;.  &#34;Straight Outta Compton&#34;, the single, is the hip hop equivalent of &#34;Concrete Jungle&#34; - young black men using music as a canvas to represent the plight of their communities and break out of their isolation.   By 1979, Bob had grown into an icon, and his vision became more prophetic.  &#34;Survival&#34; is the product of a social justice visionary gaining an international platform, one with the songwriting chops and world-class musicians to channel his message in a commercially and artistically palpable way.  One can only wonder if Hip Hop artists will transcend their genre the way Bob did, and deliver an album worthy of the praise &#34;Survival&#34; deserves.  Rap musicians should spend some time with this record before their next studio sessions and see if they can channel the same ideas through their medium.

0756405890|5.0|After being disappointed the last few times I picked up a book by a new author, I was delighted to run across this gem of a novel. Narrated in medias res by the hero himself, the author focuses on the tragic past of Kvothe the Bloodless. While the flashback is the central part of the story, this is very cleverly interspersed with action happening in the \\"present\\" as the narration is being told. While this could make the story disjointed if done by a less skilled author, it is used very effectively to generate tension and keep the reader guessing: how did the events in the story lead up to the present? Sometimes the answer is revealed, sometimes dangled just out of reach. In that respect, the story has all of the tension of a good mystery novel, but in a fantasy setting.<br /><br />I'm very much looking forward to the rest of the series.

1455502758|4.0|Without even looking at the blurbs on the book jacket of this slender volume, one might guess at some of the adjectives that appear, words like \\"poignant,\\" \\"courageous,\\" inspiring.\\" These are words that the living invariably apply to obituaries and to published accounts of a terminal disease. Such words would no doubt make Christopher Hitchens laugh. One of the best parts of Mortality is when he mocks the ubiquitous term \\"battle\\" as applied to cancer.<br /><br />And one might guess that reviewers will call Mortality \\"lucid,\\" \\"eloquent,\\" and \\"witty.\\" Well sure. That kind of writing is what made Hitchens so compulsively readable.<br /><br />Mortality is not, however, a personal account of Hitchens's last days. It is not, thank goodness, a memoir. Instead, the book is the story of how the public man known to his friends and his readers as \\"Hitch\\" thinks about the disease that will end his life. The private man, the husband, father, and friend, is hidden away, and that is how it should be.<br /><br />I have an adjective for this book. It is sad.<br /><br />But there is joy to be had in opening any of Hitchens's other books. Don't let this little tombstone of a book be your introduction to his writing. Give the man an afterlife.<br /><br />M. Feldman

Yes, it is another exploration of what makes a good and bad cop, but this time the story is so well told and the actors are so convincing that once this film has 'blitzed' by the viewer's eyes, watching it again will be an even more positive experience. Based on the novel by Ken Bruen and adapted for the screen with crackling dialogue by Nathan Parker, the story of the film is not so much one that is tough to follow - that's not the point - it is how the characters deploy themselves that makes it explode. Elliot Lester shows his mettle as an action director who understands character development and allows it to develop very naturally.<br /><br />Detective Sargeant Tom Brant (Jason Statham) has his own way of catching and punishing criminals and his quick temper and disgust for perpetrators tends to make him act quickly and violently, a trait that gets him into trouble with his superiors. A serial cop killer who goes by the name of Blitz or Barry Weiss (Aidan Gillen) is out to kill as many cops as possible because of the way Brant treated him in a pool hall brawl a few years ago. One of the cops - Chief Inspector James Roberts (Mark Rylance) is victimized and put on leave and is replaced by gay cop Porter Nash (Paddy Considine). Initially at odds because of Brant's curt put down of Nash's lifestyle, the two finally join hands, discover why they are motivated to be violent from a very similar background, and the two set out to capture the Blitz. The media gets involved with reporter Dunlop (David Morrisey) from a tip from the serial killer who really is after fame. A sidebar involves a prior undercover cop in the drug world, WPC Elizabeth Falls (Zawe Ashton), who has her own personal bruises and joins in the chase for the killer with the apparent aid of DI Craig Stokes (Luke Evans). But just when these disparate cops think they have the serial killer Blitz under control, Blitz outsmarts them again until the well-staged suspenseful chase brings the film to a surprising close.<br /><br />BLITZ is a yarn well told and well acted: Statham and Considine have great screen chemistry and the cameo roles are all molded to perfection by a very strong British cast. 0525953728|5.0|I'm going to have to change my stance on nonfiction.  I'm a very strict \\"fictionarian\\" and it takes a lot to pull me from my comfort zone.  They hype on this book did a lot to help push me over to the dark side, but the main reason was; I wanted to hear a true narrative from a real-life SEAL.  I read books by Brad Thor, Vince Flynn, Ted Bell, and Brad Taylor (to name a few) and enjoy their books immensely!  However, while their books are based on true accounts and true warrior-heroes, they aren't the real true stories.  THIS is a true story and there was no way I wasn't going to read it.  And damn, what a story!<br /><br />Let me be clear about one point from the start: I don't think \\"Owen\\" should have told this story.  My mantra is simple: \\"serve and shut the hell up\\".  Soldiers are people like the rest of us and are prone to the same mistakes we all trip over.  Sometimes we talk too damn much, hell I know I do.  But to write a book and go through the process of telling us about one of the most important missions in the history of the U.S.? Something about that doesn't sit right.  Of course in the paradoxical world in which live, I was the first one in line to buy (download) and if I had ANY conviction at all, I wouldn't be supporting the author by doing so.  Despite my feelings about \\"Owen\\" running off at the mouth, I enjoyed every minute of this book and I don't feel the least bit guilty in saying so.<br /><br />'No Easy Day' is a VERY good book!  Any story that gives you a \\"fly on the wall\\" perspective into an operation that we only hear (and only in snippets) about is a thrill.  This is extremely well written and not choppy or staccato like someone just trying to rush out a tale.  `No Easy Day' starts out with a note from the author covering his bases.  \\"This books won't tell you about any secrets\\", \\"this book has changed the names of all involved\\", \\"this book has been checked by a special attorney\\", \\"this book was endorsed by Ronald Reagan in a s&eacute;ance\\"... whatever.  If you have the stones to put out a story like this then don't bother with covering your butt.  We get it and we don't need it OK?  And the people that need an explanation won't believe anything you have to say anyway.<br /><br />After those few pages we get what we came for: the mission.  \\"Owen\\" starts off with the team being on a Blackhawk one minute out from the compound on the mission to kill Osama.  This story switches from the past to the present but not in a confusing way.  In the first part of the book we get a taste of what drove him to want to be a SEAL and the absolute agonizing vetting process these guys go through.  Physical doesn't even begin to describe it.  Grueling doesn't either.  Matter of fact; just throw out every adjective that comes to mind because you won't even come close.  These guys are the guys that Alpha Males want to be.<br /><br />We learn about SEAL training, we read about \\"Owen's\\" first missions, and we also learn about two million and one military acronyms. You wanna see what Sesame Street would be like if everyone was on crack?!  Good heavens! We are taken on missions and we see, in a small way, how SEALs run their operations, and it was a good buildup to why I wanted to read this book in the first place. \\"Owen\\" mentions more than a few times about how Hollywood gets these types of movies wrong all the time. Reading about the missions in this book I can see that. One of the best parts of this book was the mission was when they went after the pirates that kidnapped one of our. Remember that?  I do.  The precision in which they did their job is chilling. And even though I had nothing to do with that mission, I was proud. Borderline giddy.<br /><br />As I alluded to earlier, this book goes from past to present and it does so because \\"Owen\\" is setting the foundation for the big story. Bin Laden.  As \\"Owen\\" says in this book, \\"this is the mission they all dream about\\". Not necessarily Bin Laden, but one with implications that will be felt for years, even decades later. After years of tracking and misinformation, the U.S. gets lucky with a bit of Intel and that little bit turns out to be the golden ticket.  Enter the SEALs and start the death timer for that animal. In the earlier missions we didn't hear much about the political mumbo-jumbo that must be dealt with. With the hunting of this animal the American PC machine is in full useless bloom!  It's so bad the SEALs didn't even believe they would get the green light.<br /><br />One of the most sickening parts of this story was when they were planning the mission with the VIP's, and in the room with these elite, professional, war tested and war proven soldiers... was a lawyer. A freaking lawyer!!  This is a quote from this idiot: \\"If he doesn't pose a threat you will detain him\\". WHAT?!  I was literally yelling at the book, \\"this is Bin Laden you moron!!  What do you mean \\"if he poses a threat\\"?  \\"This guy's very life is a threat!!\\" Typical Washington BS.<br /><br />Stupid comments like that as well as other things done for the betterment of politics, rather than for the betterment of the mission was why the SEALs didn't believe Washington had the courage to say \\"go\\".  In this case they did and the SEALs did what they do best. This part of the book just flew by because I wanted to know what happened. OK fine, I already knew what happened, but I wanted... more.  Make time for this part of the book folks... you'll need it. I reread and highlighted more in the last 1/4 than I did the entire 3/4 of this book. Parts of this mission have their own chapter and the most arresting one was the one entitled \\"Geronimo\\". All of the (so called) controversy is worth it when you read this section. That's all I'm going to say about that.<br /><br />Because I usually read the fictional stories I had to tell myself that this was a true and these were real people.  Jen was a woman who really stood out.  She had a small part in this book but a big part in our real world.  And I know that we are talking SEAL this and SEAL that, but \\"Owen\\" paints a very detailed picture about the number of people working behind the scene. Of course SEALs usually work behind the scenes (ouch)... I've never served even as I was/am surrounded by people who do. As a layman I didn't see any secrets being given up. True I wouldn't know what to look for, but I do admit that the bigger issue is he spoke, not what he spoke about.<br /><br />Another big issue being bantered around was what \\"Owen\\" said about President Obama. Ummmmm... I finished the book and didn't see any disparaging remarks about our President. There are some remarks that were made by teammates that I'm sure were private (again, shut up \\"Owen\\") but I didn't see anything disrespectful at all. The only thing I saw were soldiers being soldiers. Soldiers, who were tired of politicians making laws from behind a desk while they put in the work. Tired of burying friends while they bury earmarks. Tired of fighting and detaining the same enemy every thirty days because they are very versed on our laws, and has taken to running and hiding under the constitution of a country they profess to hate and actively seek to destroy.  Yeah I see the frustration there and some things will be said in the heat of the moment.<br /><br />That being said, no soldier should publicly say anything negative about the Commander in Chief.  Privately... go at it.  (Holy crap, am I really at three pages for this review?)  OK I better wrap this up.  Look, bottom line; if you are looking for a quick very good non-fiction read then I highly, highly recommend this book.  Ignore the hype, and to be fair, ignore the good AND bad hype.  This is a book that you will need to read personally to draw your own conclusions.  No matter your feeling on Mr. \\"Owen\\" and his yammering jaw, we can at least agree that the SEALs are an elite and extremely brave type of soldier that America is honored to have on her soil and who will forever be in debt to these men.eal world. It is on tracks like \\"Miss You Blues\\", \\"Go, Love\\", \\"Got To Have Something\\", and \\"Bluebird\\" that it was on display and bothered me.<br /><br />The folk songs fared much better in my book. In addition, I liked how Knopfler used interesting structure in places such as \\"Blood And Water\\" which follows a chorus/verse/chorus/verse/chorus pattern.  This is inverted compared to what the radio-hit formula suggests.  The closing song \\"After The Beanstalk\\" is a honky tonk number that will appeal to O' Brother, Where Art Thou? fans.<br /><br />Overall, Privateering is a collection of songs that spans a number of styles and has a lot to like.  You don't need to be a long time die-hard Mark Knopfler fan to find something here to enjoy.  Give it a try. B0010SKUYM|4.0|Patrick Rothfuss takes the much accepted stereotype of the 'reluctant' dark hero to new heights with amazing levels of reflection and depth of character of the protagonist and the dark hero.. Kote aka kvothe. Along with the protagonist, he has created a supercast of other intriguing and interesting characters in his book 'The Name of the Wind'; the first of a trilogy. The book traces the emergence of the hero Kvothe from his rather benign beginnings as a kid born in the travelling troupe of performers... the 'Edemah Ruh'<br /><br />Introduced in the current time as a staid in-keeper wanting to stay away from troubles and situations demanding action, the story of Kvothe is revealed layer by intriguing layer through the hero's narration to the storyteller aka 'The Chronicler'. Bast, the 'companion-friend' of Kvothe... is used cleverly as a counterpoint to reveal hidden aspects of Kvothe through Bast's perception.<br /><br />Never before has a fantasy hero been given so many facets of character.. warrior, musician, magician. Instead of diluting, each of these facets re-inforce each other to give the character of Kvothe complicated, but solid mass.<br /><br />The myth of the dark cult of chandrian, the narration of the childhood of Kvothe among the troupe of Edema Ruh and his tenuous romance with the now appearing/ now disappearing mysterious character of the girl Denna are some of the meaty dimensions which alternate in the story telling. The best about this is , whenever the author breaks one of the narrative dimensions (the upbringing among the Edema Ruh)to enter another.. the reader continues to be hooked to the narrative.<br /><br />Equally endearing is the author's picturisation and characterisation of the University, its teachers and  Kvothe's fellow students. All very nicely blending with the storyline and the epoch.<br /><br />Overall, a wonderful read and enough depth and material to leave the reader with a satisfied but 'wanting more' feeling...

B003JTHWJQ|2.0|I read this book as part of a study group that focuses on science and religion.  The group consists of some atheists, Christians, and me, the token Mormon.  Many of us are well educated and are engineers and scientists, but cover other areas of expertise.  We have covered many books on Christianity, science, and philosophy.  This was our first dive into a book on atheism.<br /><br />Not surprisingly, we were split on how good the book was.  We all agreed that Dawkins was a good writer and effectively pointed out weaknesses in religion and professed religious people.  We also agreed that he tended to focus on his favorite target of Fundamentalist Christians.  Since none of us are currently `Fundamentalist Christians' we agreed with him on many of his points, especially his attacks on people that reject science in the name of religion.  We also noted that many of his examples of bad behaviors by religious people are also duplicated in the name of race, nationalism, politics or differences in general.<br /><br />The bottom line is that the points he brought up to `prove' that there is no God, simply shot down some very poor proofs of God and prove that human nature is exhibited in religion as well as many other areas that he did not mention.  I would have liked to have seen some statistics backing up his claims that religious people are worse than non-religious people.  We all came up with many examples on our own of atheistic people doing the same sort of bad things that religious people do.<br /><br />One of the good things that Dawkins did point out was that atheists can be good without a belief in God.  This is a genuine problem that he highlights very effectively, and no one should feel that they are morally superior to another due to their belief or lack thereof in God.  As a side note, Mormon's and Christians do teach that there is a universal `conscience' or `light of Christ' that teaches everyone right from wrong.<br /><br />Now for the `Mormon' perspective.  Dawkins attacks a God that is nothing at all like the God that I believe in.  I believe in a God that is a Supreme Being that has mastered the laws of science and uses them for His purposes including the creation.  Therefore, there is no conflict in the existence of God and the fact that evolution can be explained by science.  I see God as the farmer that plants the seeds of a world and waits for it to be ready for intelligent life.  God creates by organizing existing elements which are co-eternal with Him.  We believe that God did not create matter out of nothing (as most Christians, Jews, and Muslims believe), but brings organization out of chaos.  Dawkins arguments assume that God created everything out of nothing.<br /><br />Dawkins obviously understands evolution, and even admits that the beginning of life has not been explained by science, but goes with the fact that probabilities would eventually create the right conditions and create life (the anthropic principle).  He is saying that the fact that this highly improbable event happened here on earth can be explained by the point that there are unnumbered worlds on which this could happen and it was bound to happen somewhere, sometime.  I do not find that convincing given the complexity of even the simplest forms of life.<br /><br />I also agreed with Dawkins on another point; that if God existed, that people that follow Him should behave better than those that don't follow Him.  I also am disturbed by people that claim to follow God, but do horrible things in His name.  Christ also had a serious problem with hypocrites.  Dawkins pointed out the hypocrites, but failed to list the many examples of religious people that practice what they preach.<br /><br />In general, I enjoyed reading this book and it brought out many interesting conversations in our group.  I think that if he had retitled the book `The Fundamentalist Christian Delusion', that it would have reflected much better what he was preaching.Dawkins attacks a God that is nothing at all like the God that I believe in. I do not find that convincing given the complexity of even the simplest forms of life.<br /><br />I also agreed with Dawkins on another point; that if God existed, that people that follow Him should behave better than those that don't follow Him. B003K16PFU|3.0|George Orwell's \\"Homage to Catalonia\\" is an interesting book describing his experiences fighting in the Spanish Civil War.  These experiences are what turned him away from Stalinistic Communism.  One thing to remember while reading this book is that while Orwell rejects Stalin and Communism, he only switches camps to Socialism.  If you think he becomes Conservative (in the American sense) during this event, then you'll be confused by his world-view in this book.  His basic assumptions of how the world should work sound like those of a freshman Political Science student at a radical university (i.e., extremely left-wing) and will leave you scratching your head trying to figure out what he's talking about.  So, that's one source of possible confusion while reading the book.  Another is inherent in the event itself.  This is not a chronicle of two sides fighting.  Instead, it's a chronicle of many sides fighting for and against each other (at least Communists, Socialists, Anarchists, the \\"government,\\" and Fascists (Franco)).  Frankly, he doesn't doesn't do a very good job of explaining all of it.  There's very little background explaining what led to the Revolution and reading many of his pages is like looking at a bowl of alphabet soup:  it's just a sea of meaningless acronyms floating around.  Another issue is that he doesn't intersperse his chronicling of personal events with his political explanations very well:  his political asides go on for scores of pages and totally disrupt the flow of the book.  So, even though it's a very interesting book, I have to subtract a star for the technical implementation and give it merely an OK 3 stars out of 5.

I know reviews don't mean anything but the reviews on Amazon agree with my personal preference when it comes to Rush. The 4 1/2 - 5 star rated albums are absolutely my favorites (with Counterparts being the only exception to the rule).<br /><br />I would honestly propose that Clockwork Angels is the best album since Signals. To me Rush has finally found the perfect balance between what was, what is, and what can be with Clockwork Angels. The musicianship is at an all time high, Lee has finally found a balance between his vocal comfort zone and belting it out like he did in the 70's, Lifeson is given a chance to shine on several tracks providing some well crafted solos, Peart is as good as ever (despite his track being just a bit too quiet), and most importantly this album feels like Rush. Let me explain that last point a bit.<br /><br />In my opinion Test For Echo, Vapor Trails and Snakes & Arrows didn't really have the same flavor as previous Rush albums. Don't get me wrong they are still good albums. There were parts that sounded like Rush, however on a whole the albums were off when compared the previous entries. Clockwork Angels, on the other hand, has a feel that is much closer to what Rush sounded like in the 70's, 80's and early 90's. I know this point is very qualitative but there has to be at least one Rush fan out there that understands what I am talking about.<br /><br />So, whats the only thing this album desperately needs? Extra care during the mixing process. The mixing is good (tweaking the equalizer to the exact perfect values can help a bit) but could be better. Put on a decent pair of headphones, sit back and relax, close your eyes and listen to The Camera Eye, Freewill, or Analog Kid (and many more songs) and you can easily spot the difference (I'm completely inept when it comes to sound quality and even I can tell a difference) between the the past and the present when it comes to production value. The Wreckers and The Garden were instantly put into my Rush Top 20 songs list but they can sound obnoxious when listening at loud volumes.<br /><br />Rush needs to run with the momentum gained from this album and put more emphasis on production values in order to get an album that is dubbed \\"best album since Moving Pictures\\" rather than \\"best album since Signals\\".e, Freewill, or Analog Kid (and many more songs) and you can easily spot the difference (I'm completely inept when it comes to sound quality and even I can tell a difference) between the the past and the present when it comes to production value.
